Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Rational Proof for God's Life
Sanusi gives a conditional proof for life's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical conditional proof that necessitates God's attribute of life based on other proven attributes.

Core Idea
Life is necessary because without it, no other attribute can exist.

This lesson presents the first part of the source, where Sanusi constructs a rational proof for God's attribute of life. The argument uses a conditional: if life were unnecessary, then other attributes like power and knowledge would be impossible. Since those attributes are already proven necessary, life must also be necessary. This is a foundational argument in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما الكلام' to 'فيثبت كونه تعالى واجبا له الاتصاف بالحياة.' Identify the 'if... then... but... therefore' structure.
Notice that the proof relies on earlier proofs for other attributes.
2
Identify the logical form
The argument is: (1) If God were not living, He could not have power, will, knowledge, etc. (2) He does have these attributes (proven earlier). Therefore, He must be living. This is a modus tollens in disguise.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself: why is life considered a prerequisite for other attributes? The answer lies in the definition of 'living' as that which perceives and acts. Without life, agency is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - صفة الحياة، تعريفها، ودليل وجوبها له تعالى
وأما الكلام على دليل وجوب اتصافه الله بالحياة فنقول : لو لم يجب أن يتصف بالحياة لما صح أنْ يتّصفَ بالقدرة والإرادة والعلم وغيرها من الصفات، لكن اتصافه تعالى بهذه الصفات ثابت بالدليل الذي أقمناه على وجوب اتصافه بكل واحدة منها ، فيثبت كونه تعالى واجبا له الاتصاف بالحياة.
قال حجة الإسلام الغزالي: واتصافه تعالى بالحياة معلوم بالضرورة، ولم ينكره أحدٌ ممَّن اعترَفَ بكونه تعالى عالماً ، قادرًا ، فإنَّ كونَ العالِم القادرِ حيًّا ضروري ؛ إذ لا نعني بالحي إلا ما يشعر بنفسه ويعلم ذاته وغيره، والعالم بجميع المعلومات، والقادر على جميع المقدورات، كيف لا يكون حيًّا ؟! وهذا واضح والنَّظر في صفة الحياة لا يطول» اهـ . [ انظر: في الاعتقاد للحجة الغزالي ، ص١٦٧]
4 – The attribute of life: its definition and the proof of its necessity for Him, exalted. As for the discussion on the proof of the necessity of His being described by life, we say: If it were not necessary for Him to be described by life, then it would not be valid for Him to be described by power, will, knowledge, and other attributes. But His being described by these attributes is established by the proof we have presented for the necessity of His being described by each one of them. So it is established that He, exalted, necessarily has the attribute of life. Al-Ghazali, the proof of Islam, said: His being described by life is known necessarily, and no one who acknowledges that He is knowing and powerful has denied it. For the knowing and powerful being is necessarily living; we mean by 'living' nothing but what perceives itself and knows its essence and others, and He is knower of all known things and powerful over all possible things—how could He not be living?! This is clear, and the investigation into the attribute of life is not lengthy. End quote. [See: Al-I'tiqad by al-Ghazali, p. 167]
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for the necessity of the attribute of life for God. It begins by stating that if life were not necessary for God, then the other attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.) could not be validly attributed to Him, because these attributes require a living subject. Since those attributes have already been proven necessary by earlier arguments, life must also be necessary. The proof is thus a conditional argument (if not life, then no other attributes; but other attributes are proven; therefore life). The passage then reinforces this with a quote from al-Ghazali, who argues that life is known intuitively (bi-ḍarūrah) from the fact that God is knowing and powerful, because 'living' means that which perceives itself and knows its essence and others. Since God knows all and is powerful over all, He must be living. The argument is straightforward and concise.

Key Terms
الحياة
وجوب
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of the proof for God's attribute of life according to this passage?
A) Life is proven directly from scripture.
B) If life were not necessary, then other proven attributes would be impossible; therefore life is necessary.
C) Life is proven by consensus of all theologians.
D) Life is not a real attribute but a metaphor.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of God's life is proven rationally from the necessity of other attributes like power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's very possession of power and knowledge demands that He be living, then what does your possession of any spiritual virtue require? Is your faith, knowledge, or worship alive, or have you let it become mechanical? Today, will you ask the Living God to revive your heart so that every act of obedience springs from life?
?
Sanusi proves that without life, no other attribute can exist. Reflect on your inner state: are there areas of your life where you try to produce good deeds or knowledge without first seeking life from Allah? What would change if you prioritized spiritual vitality—living connection with Allah—over mere actions?
?
The proof shows that life is not optional for God; it is necessary. But for you, living faith is not automatic—it requires seeking and nurturing. Will you commit to one concrete practice today (such as heartfelt dhikr or reading the Qur'an with presence) to ensure your faith remains alive, not just a set of beliefs?

Al-Ghazali's Intuitive Argument for Life
Al-Ghazali argues life is intuitively known from knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's argument that God's life is known intuitively (bi-ḍarūrah) from His knowledge and power.

Core Idea
God's life is self-evident to anyone who admits His knowledge and power.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the source, where Al-Ghazali reinforces the proof by appealing to intuitive knowledge. He argues that no one who acknowledges God's knowledge and power can deny His life, because the concept of 'living' is inherent in the idea of a knowing, powerful agent. This argument is presented as a clear and necessary truth.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ghazali's statement
Read the passage from 'قال حجة الإسلام الغزالي' to the end. Notice how he claims life is known by necessity and that no one denies it.
Al-Ghazali is known for emphasizing intuitive knowledge (ḍarūrah).
2
Analyze the definition of 'living'
Al-Ghazali defines 'living' as that which perceives itself and knows itself and others. Since God knows all things, He must be living by definition.
3
Connect to the overall proof
Understand that this intuitive argument complements the rational proof. While Sanusi used a conditional, al-Ghazali uses a direct conceptual link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - صفة الحياة، تعريفها، ودليل وجوبها له تعالى
وأما الكلام على دليل وجوب اتصافه الله بالحياة فنقول : لو لم يجب أن يتصف بالحياة لما صح أنْ يتّصفَ بالقدرة والإرادة والعلم وغيرها من الصفات، لكن اتصافه تعالى بهذه الصفات ثابت بالدليل الذي أقمناه على وجوب اتصافه بكل واحدة منها ، فيثبت كونه تعالى واجبا له الاتصاف بالحياة.
قال حجة الإسلام الغزالي: واتصافه تعالى بالحياة معلوم بالضرورة، ولم ينكره أحدٌ ممَّن اعترَفَ بكونه تعالى عالماً ، قادرًا ، فإنَّ كونَ العالِم القادرِ حيًّا ضروري ؛ إذ لا نعني بالحي إلا ما يشعر بنفسه ويعلم ذاته وغيره، والعالم بجميع المعلومات، والقادر على جميع المقدورات، كيف لا يكون حيًّا ؟! وهذا واضح والنَّظر في صفة الحياة لا يطول» اهـ . [ انظر: في الاعتقاد للحجة الغزالي ، ص١٦٧]
4 – The attribute of life: its definition and the proof of its necessity for Him, exalted. As for the discussion on the proof of the necessity of His being described by life, we say: If it were not necessary for Him to be described by life, then it would not be valid for Him to be described by power, will, knowledge, and other attributes. But His being described by these attributes is established by the proof we have presented for the necessity of His being described by each one of them. So it is established that He, exalted, necessarily has the attribute of life. Al-Ghazali, the proof of Islam, said: His being described by life is known necessarily, and no one who acknowledges that He is knowing and powerful has denied it. For the knowing and powerful being is necessarily living; we mean by 'living' nothing but what perceives itself and knows its essence and others, and He is knower of all known things and powerful over all possible things—how could He not be living?! This is clear, and the investigation into the attribute of life is not lengthy. End quote. [See: Al-I'tiqad by al-Ghazali, p. 167]
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for the necessity of the attribute of life for God. It begins by stating that if life were not necessary for God, then the other attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.) could not be validly attributed to Him, because these attributes require a living subject. Since those attributes have already been proven necessary by earlier arguments, life must also be necessary. The proof is thus a conditional argument (if not life, then no other attributes; but other attributes are proven; therefore life). The passage then reinforces this with a quote from al-Ghazali, who argues that life is known intuitively (bi-ḍarūrah) from the fact that God is knowing and powerful, because 'living' means that which perceives itself and knows its essence and others. Since God knows all and is powerful over all, He must be living. The argument is straightforward and concise.

Key Terms
الحياة
ضرورة
الغزالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's life is only known through revelation and not rationally.
Correction: Al-Ghazali argues it is known necessarily (intuitively) from the fact that God is knowing and powerful.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, why is God's life known necessarily?
A) Because scripture explicitly states it.
B) Because anyone who accepts God's knowledge and power must accept His life, as living is defined by self-perception.
C) Because it has been proven by consensus.
D) Because life is a metaphor for activity.

Key Takeaway
God's life is an intuitive necessity derived from His knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's life means He perceives and knows all, including your secret thoughts, how does this change the way you live your private moments? What will you commit to change today?
?
Al-Ghazali says no one who acknowledges God's knowledge denies His life. But do you truly live as if God knows and perceives you? Examine one area where you act as if He does not see. What will you do to align with this truth?
?
The definition of living is 'what perceives itself'. God knows His own essence perfectly. Do you know yourself in light of His knowledge? What is one step you can take to know yourself better before your Creator?

Life's Unique Lack of Relation
Sanusi states and explains the exception of life from ta'alluq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life is unique among God's attributes in having no relation (ta'alluq), and how it differs from attributes like power and will.

Core Idea
Life stands alone—it needs no object to act upon.

This lesson zooms in on a key distinction in Ash'ari theology: while attributes like power and will inherently relate to objects, life does not. The passage explains the reasoning behind this and even compares life to other atemporal attributes like pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the core claim
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi states that life has no ta'alluq and gives the reason: it does not entail an extra matter beyond its subsistence.
Notice the contrast with power and will, which require an object.
2
Understand the reason
Highlight the rationale: life is complete with just its subsistence; it doesn't demand a second thing like an object of power or will.
This is a simple but profound point.
3
Compare with other attributes
Sanusi says all other attributes of meanings (sifāt al-maʿānī) except life have a ta'alluq. List them: power, will, knowledge, etc.
What do they all have in common that life lacks?
4
Note the analogue
Sanusi says pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā') are like life in having no ta'alluq, if considered attributes of meanings.
This shows that the concept isn't unique to life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن هذه الصفة تتعلق بشيء أو لا تتعلق ؟ فنقول: إن صفة الحياة لا تتعلق بشيء، وذلك لأنها لا تستلزم أمراً زائداً على القيام بمحلها ، والصِّفةُ التي تتعلق بشيءٍ هي التي تستلزم أمرًا بعد قيامه بمحله ـ يطلب أمرًا يُعلَم به ؟ وكذا الاقتصاد زائداً على القيام بمحلها ، ألا ترى أنَّ القدرة والإرادة ونحوهما. العلم - ومن الذي قدمناه في ذكر ما تتعلق به الصفاتُ تَعلَم أَنَّ جميعَ صِفات المعاني متعلّقة، أي: طالبةٌ لزائد على القيام بمحالها ، إلَّا الحياة ؛ فإنها لا تتعلق بشيء، ونظيرُ الحياة القدم والبقاء عند من يجعلهما من صفات المعاني، وهذا التعلق نفسي لتلك الصفات، فلا توجد في صفة منها بدون تعلّقها، فتعلّقُ كلَّ صفةٍ بما تتعلّق به أزلي ، وكذلك قيام كل صفة من الخارج منها بالذات نفسي، فلا توجد صفةٌ منها في الخارج قائمة بنفسها، بل توجد قائمة بالذَّاتِ، وما ذكرناه مِن أنَّ التَّعلَّقَ نفسي هو قول الأشعري ، وقيل : إنَّ كلا من تعلق الصفة وقيامها بالذات أمر اعتباري ، وإنه من قبيل النّسب والإضافات ، وقيل : إنه من مواقف العقول، أي: لا يعلمه إلا الله ، وقيل : إن تعلّق الصفة صفةٌ وجوديَّةٌ لها ، ورُدَّ هذا بأنَّه يستلزم قيامَ المعنَى بالمعنَى . واعلم أنَّ التَّعلَّق الموصوفَ بكونه نفسيَّا هو التَّعلَّق القديم، لا الحادث، لتحقق الصفة أزلا وأبدا بدون التعلق الحادث، والتّعلُّق القديم يشمل التنجيزي القديم بالنسبة للعلم والإرادة والكلام، ويشمل الصلوحي القديم بالنسبة للقدرة والإرادة ، وليس خاصا بالصلوحي خلافا لبعضهم . محي .
As for whether this attribute is related to something or not: we say that the attribute of life is not related to anything, because it does not entail any additional matter beyond its subsistence in its locus. The attribute that is related to something is that which entails a matter after its subsistence in its locus—it demands a matter to be known? Likewise al-iqtisād [economy?] additional beyond its subsistence in its locus. Do you not see that power, will, and the like, and knowledge? And from what we have presented in mentioning what the attributes are related to, you know that all attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) are related, i.e., seeking an additional matter beyond their subsistence in their loci, except life, for it is not related to anything. The analogue of life is pre-eternity (al-qidam) and everlastingness (al-baqāʾ) according to those who make them attributes of meanings. This relation is intrinsic (nafsī) for those attributes, so none of them exists without its relation. Thus the relation of each attribute to what it is related to is eternal, and likewise the subsistence of each attribute externally from its essence is intrinsic (nafsī); so no attribute exists in external reality subsisting by itself, but rather it exists subsisting in the essence. What we have mentioned—that the relation is intrinsic—is the view of al-Ashʿarī. And it is said: both the relation of the attribute and its subsistence in the essence are a matter of mental consideration (iʿtibārī), and are of the category of relations and additions. And it is said: it is from the stations of intellects, i.e., only God knows it. And it is said: the relation of the attribute is an existential attribute of it, and this was refuted because it entails the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning. Know that the relation described as intrinsic is the eternal relation, not the temporal, because the attribute is realized eternally and forever without the temporal relation. The eternal relation includes the determinative eternal (al-tanjīzī al-qadīm) with respect to knowledge, will, and speech, and includes the aptitudinal eternal (al-ṣalūḥī al-qadīm) with respect to power and will, and it is not specific to the aptitudinal, contrary to some. End.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage examines whether the attribute of life has a relation (taʿalluq) to something. It first asserts that life has no relation because it does not require an extra matter beyond its subsistence in the essence, unlike attributes like power and will which require an object. It then explains that all attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) except life have a taʿalluq that is intrinsic (nafsī)—meaning it is inseparable from the attribute and eternal. The discussion then lists four opinions: (1) the Ashʿarī view that taʿalluq is nafsī; (2) that taʿalluq and subsistence are mental considerations (iʿtibārī); (3) that it is a 'station of intellects' known only to God; (4) that taʿalluq is an existential attribute of the attribute (refuted as causing a meaning to subsist in a meaning). Finally, it distinguishes the eternal taʿalluq (al-qadīm) from temporal (al-ḥādith), and breaks the eternal into two types: determinative (tanzīzī) for knowledge, will, and speech; and aptitudinal (ṣalūḥī) for power and will, clarifying that it is not exclusive to the aptitudinal.

Key Terms
تعلق
نفسي
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes have a relation to an object.
Correction: Life, pre-eternity, and everlastingness are attributes that do not have a relation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the attribute of life has no relation (ta'alluq)?
A) Because life is not a real attribute.
B) Because life does not require any additional matter beyond its subsistence in the essence.
C) Because life is created in time.
D) Because life is dependent on other attributes.

Key Takeaway
Life is unique among the attributes of meanings in having no relation to anything, because it is complete in its subsistence without needing an object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah is Living. But do you live as though His life is dependent on your actions or worship? This lesson shows His life is complete without any relation to you. How does that challenge your tendency to think your deeds ‘add’ something to Him? What would change if you truly internalized that Allah’s life is utterly self-sufficient?
?
If life that needs no object is the most perfect, why do you constantly seek life from things that are themselves lifeless? This lesson forces you to examine where you seek your identity, security, and purpose—from relationships, achievements, or from the One whose life is uncaused. Take a moment: name one created thing you have acted as though it gives you life. Now, how can you transfer that reliance to Allah?
?
Sanusi teaches that life is unique among the attributes because it requires nothing outside itself. Does your relationship with Allah reflect that reality? Or do you treat Him as though He needs your praise or obedience? This lesson is a mirror: the less you feel you ‘need’ from Allah, the more you might be missing that He is the source of all life. Commit to a specific practice this week that reminds you of your absolute need for Him, but His independence from you.

The Nature and Types of Ta'alluq
Sanusi discusses the nature of ta'alluq and its eternal/temporal types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different views on the nature of ta'alluq (intrinsic, mental, or existential) and the distinction between eternal and temporal relations, as well as the two types of eternal relation.

Core Idea
Ta'alluq is intrinsic for Ash'arī, eternal, and includes determinative and aptitudinal types.

After establishing that most attributes have a relation, Sanusi delves into the nature of that relation. He presents the dominant Ash'arī view that ta'alluq is intrinsic (nafsī) and eternal, then surveys alternative positions. Finally, he unpacks the eternal relation into two subtypes: determinative (tanzīzī) for knowledge, will, and speech, and aptitudinal (ṣalūḥī) for power and will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'arī view
Read the passage from 'This relation is intrinsic' to 'the view of al-Ashʿarī'. Note that ta'alluq is inseparable from the attribute and eternal.
This is the mainstream position.
2
Survey the other opinions
The text lists three other views: (1) ta'alluq and subsistence are mental considerations (iʿtibārī), (2) they are unknown except to God, (3) ta'alluq is an existential attribute (refuted).
Which view is refuted and why?
3
Distinguish eternal from temporal ta'alluq
Sanusi clarifies that the intrinsic ta'alluq is eternal, not temporal, because the attribute exists without a temporal relation.
Temporal relations are not part of the essence.
4
Learn the two subtypes of eternal ta'alluq
The eternal relation is either determinative (tanzīzī) for knowledge, will, speech or aptitudinal (ṣalūḥī) for power and will. Note that will appears in both but is not limited to one type.
What does this say about the flexibility of will?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن هذه الصفة تتعلق بشيء أو لا تتعلق ؟ فنقول: إن صفة الحياة لا تتعلق بشيء، وذلك لأنها لا تستلزم أمراً زائداً على القيام بمحلها ، والصِّفةُ التي تتعلق بشيءٍ هي التي تستلزم أمرًا بعد قيامه بمحله ـ يطلب أمرًا يُعلَم به ؟ وكذا الاقتصاد زائداً على القيام بمحلها ، ألا ترى أنَّ القدرة والإرادة ونحوهما. العلم - ومن الذي قدمناه في ذكر ما تتعلق به الصفاتُ تَعلَم أَنَّ جميعَ صِفات المعاني متعلّقة، أي: طالبةٌ لزائد على القيام بمحالها ، إلَّا الحياة ؛ فإنها لا تتعلق بشيء، ونظيرُ الحياة القدم والبقاء عند من يجعلهما من صفات المعاني، وهذا التعلق نفسي لتلك الصفات، فلا توجد في صفة منها بدون تعلّقها، فتعلّقُ كلَّ صفةٍ بما تتعلّق به أزلي ، وكذلك قيام كل صفة من الخارج منها بالذات نفسي، فلا توجد صفةٌ منها في الخارج قائمة بنفسها، بل توجد قائمة بالذَّاتِ، وما ذكرناه مِن أنَّ التَّعلَّقَ نفسي هو قول الأشعري ، وقيل : إنَّ كلا من تعلق الصفة وقيامها بالذات أمر اعتباري ، وإنه من قبيل النّسب والإضافات ، وقيل : إنه من مواقف العقول، أي: لا يعلمه إلا الله ، وقيل : إن تعلّق الصفة صفةٌ وجوديَّةٌ لها ، ورُدَّ هذا بأنَّه يستلزم قيامَ المعنَى بالمعنَى . واعلم أنَّ التَّعلَّق الموصوفَ بكونه نفسيَّا هو التَّعلَّق القديم، لا الحادث، لتحقق الصفة أزلا وأبدا بدون التعلق الحادث، والتّعلُّق القديم يشمل التنجيزي القديم بالنسبة للعلم والإرادة والكلام، ويشمل الصلوحي القديم بالنسبة للقدرة والإرادة ، وليس خاصا بالصلوحي خلافا لبعضهم . محي .
As for whether this attribute is related to something or not: we say that the attribute of life is not related to anything, because it does not entail any additional matter beyond its subsistence in its locus. The attribute that is related to something is that which entails a matter after its subsistence in its locus—it demands a matter to be known? Likewise al-iqtisād [economy?] additional beyond its subsistence in its locus. Do you not see that power, will, and the like, and knowledge? And from what we have presented in mentioning what the attributes are related to, you know that all attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) are related, i.e., seeking an additional matter beyond their subsistence in their loci, except life, for it is not related to anything. The analogue of life is pre-eternity (al-qidam) and everlastingness (al-baqāʾ) according to those who make them attributes of meanings. This relation is intrinsic (nafsī) for those attributes, so none of them exists without its relation. Thus the relation of each attribute to what it is related to is eternal, and likewise the subsistence of each attribute externally from its essence is intrinsic (nafsī); so no attribute exists in external reality subsisting by itself, but rather it exists subsisting in the essence. What we have mentioned—that the relation is intrinsic—is the view of al-Ashʿarī. And it is said: both the relation of the attribute and its subsistence in the essence are a matter of mental consideration (iʿtibārī), and are of the category of relations and additions. And it is said: it is from the stations of intellects, i.e., only God knows it. And it is said: the relation of the attribute is an existential attribute of it, and this was refuted because it entails the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning. Know that the relation described as intrinsic is the eternal relation, not the temporal, because the attribute is realized eternally and forever without the temporal relation. The eternal relation includes the determinative eternal (al-tanjīzī al-qadīm) with respect to knowledge, will, and speech, and includes the aptitudinal eternal (al-ṣalūḥī al-qadīm) with respect to power and will, and it is not specific to the aptitudinal, contrary to some. End.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage examines whether the attribute of life has a relation (taʿalluq) to something. It first asserts that life has no relation because it does not require an extra matter beyond its subsistence in the essence, unlike attributes like power and will which require an object. It then explains that all attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) except life have a taʿalluq that is intrinsic (nafsī)—meaning it is inseparable from the attribute and eternal. The discussion then lists four opinions: (1) the Ashʿarī view that taʿalluq is nafsī; (2) that taʿalluq and subsistence are mental considerations (iʿtibārī); (3) that it is a 'station of intellects' known only to God; (4) that taʿalluq is an existential attribute of the attribute (refuted as causing a meaning to subsist in a meaning). Finally, it distinguishes the eternal taʿalluq (al-qadīm) from temporal (al-ḥādith), and breaks the eternal into two types: determinative (tanzīzī) for knowledge, will, and speech; and aptitudinal (ṣalūḥī) for power and will, clarifying that it is not exclusive to the aptitudinal.

D) Aptitudinal (ṣalūḥī) and mental (iʿtibārī)

Key Takeaway
The ta'alluq of attributes is intrinsic and eternal for Ash'arī, and it is divided into determinative and aptitudinal types.

?
The passage refutes the idea that ta'alluq is an existential attribute because it would lead to an impossibility. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that, upon inspection, lead to logical absurdities? Are you willing to subject your personal views to the same rigorous thinking that Sanusi demands?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between determinative and aptitudinal relations for different attributes. In your own understanding, do you distinguish between what God has actually decreed and what He is capable of? How does this distinction affect your trust in His wisdom and power when you face uncertainty?

Definition and Proof of Speech as a Divine Attribute
Sanusi defines God's speech as an eternal attribute proven by revelation and explains its relationship to scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines God's attribute of speech as an eternal attribute proven by revelation, and how it relates to the revealed scriptures.

Core Idea
God's speech is one eternal attribute; scriptures are its expressions.

Sanusi now turns to the fifth divine attribute: speech. Unlike the previous attributes, this one is proven by revelation (samʿ) rather than reason. The passage defines speech as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, and clarifies how the Quran, Gospel, and Torah are different expressions of the same single attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without focusing on footnotes. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own structure come through.
Notice the footnote markers (1-4); they are explained in the commentary.
2
Identify the mode of proof
The first sentence states that speech is proven by revelation (dahl al-samʿ) unlike the previous attributes which had rational proof. This is a key distinction.
Kalam divides attributes into those known by reason and those known by revelation alone.
3
Understand the definition
The second sentence defines speech as an eternal attribute opposite to silence and incapacity. Through it, God commands, prohibits, informs, etc.
Opposition to silence is deliberate; incapacity is inability.
4
Grasp the unity of scripture
The passage explains that when expressed in different languages, the Quran, Gospel, and Torah result, but they all refer to the same eternal attribute.
This guards against equating the created book with the uncreated speech.

Source Text

(۱) قوله: (منافية للسكوت والآفة السكوتُ ترك الكلام اختيارا، والآفة عجز . الأمير.
(۲) قوله: (والإشارة) يحتمل أن يكون المراد الإشارة الحسية بحيث يُشار إلى النقوش، بأن يقال : هذا كلام الله ، فمدلول اسم الإشارة المعنى بواسطتين. ويصح أن يراد بها الإشارة العقلية ، بأن يُرجع في قلبه الإشارة ويلاحظ أن المرجع المعنى القائم بذاته ; فالمراد بالعبارة: الكتب المنزلة، والإشارة : لفظ نستعمله نحن ، كأن نقول ذلك المعنى القائم بالذات قديم، ويكفي في الإشارة الشعور بوجه ما . الشنواني والأمير.
(۳) قوله: (فإن عبر عنها أي عن بعض مدلولها. الأمير.
(٤) قوله فالمسمى (واحد أراد به المدلول بمعنى الصفة القديمة. الأمير.
Section on Divine Matters
01 – The attribute of speech: its definition and the aspect of its proof for God, exalted.
Thus speech is the fifth attribute; it is like the previous attributes in the necessity of being described by it, though it differs from them in the aspect of proof: for it has the proof of revelation (samʿ) while they have the proof of reason (ʿaql).
It is: an eternal attribute, subsisting in His essence, opposed to silence and incapacity (1). Through it He is commander, prohibitor, informant, etc. It is indicated by expression, writing, and indication (2). If expressed (3) in Arabic, it is the Quran; in Syriac, the Gospel; in Hebrew, the Torah. The thing named is one (4) even if the expressions differ. This is the meaning of his words (h).
(1) His words: 'opposed to silence and incapacity' – silence is the abandonment of speech by choice, incapacity is inability. al-Amir.
(2) His words: 'and indication' – it is possible that what is meant is sensory indication, such that one points to the inscriptions, saying 'this is the speech of God,' so the referent of the demonstrative pronoun is the meaning with two intermediaries. It is also possible that what is meant is intellectual indication, such that one turns the indication in one's heart and notes that the referent is the meaning subsisting in the essence. So what is meant by 'expression' is the revealed books, and by 'indication' is a term we use, like saying 'that meaning subsisting in the essence is eternal.' For indication, a certain awareness suffices. al-Shanawani and al-Amir.
(3) His words: 'if expressed' – i.e., some of its content. al-Amir.
(4) His words: 'the thing named is one' – he meant the referent, i.e., the eternal attribute. al-Amir.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of speech (al-kalām) and states its mode of proof. It begins by noting that speech is the fifth attribute and, like the previous ones, its attribution to God is necessary, but unlike them it is proven by revelation (samʿ) rather than reason (ʿaql). The definition follows: it is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, opposite to silence and incapacity; through it God commands, prohibits, informs, etc. It is indicated by expression (ʿibārah), writing (kitābah), and indication (ishārah). When expressed in different languages, the result is the Quran, Gospel, or Torah, but the referent is one – the single eternal attribute. The footnotes (from the commentary) clarify: (1) silence is deliberate non-speech, incapacity is inability; (2) 'indication' can be sensory (pointing to written text) or intellectual (inner awareness of the meaning); (3) 'expressed' refers to part of its content; (4) 'the thing named' is the eternal attribute itself.

The Meaning of God's Speech: Ahl al-Sunna vs. Mu'tazila
Sanusi contrasts the Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila definitions of God's speech as the first of three aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting definitions of God's speech held by Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila, and the key negations in the Sunni definition.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal and not composed of letters or sounds.

This lesson introduces the first of three aspects of the attribute of divine speech: its meaning. Imam Sanusi presents the Sunni definition as an eternal attribute free from physical and psychological limitations, contrasting it with the Mu'tazila view of created letters and sounds. Although the passage is incomplete, the doctrinal contrast is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the three-aspect framework
Notice that Sanusi first outlines three aspects of divine speech: meaning, proof, and relation. This lesson covers only the first aspect.
The other two aspects are treated in later lessons.
2
Examine the Sunni definition
Read the Ahl al-Sunna definition carefully, noting each negation: not a letter or sound, not subject to time, grammar, psychological silence, or incapacity.
This definition emphasizes God's speech is unlike human speech.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila definition
The Mu'tazila define speech as created (hadith) letters and sounds that do not subsist in God's essence. Compare this with the Sunni view.
The passage cuts off after presenting the Mu'tazila position.

(5) The discussion on the attribute of speech relates to it from several aspects: first: explaining the meaning of speech with respect to Him; second: the proof of the necessity of God being described by speech; third: explaining what the attribute of speech is related to. As for the first aspect, we say: The people of religions and sects differed regarding the meaning of His speech. Ahl al-Sunna said: speech is 'an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, not a letter or a sound, transcendent before and after, transcendent of declension and construction, transcendent of psychological silence in that He does not internally formulate speech despite ability, and transcendent of inner incapacity such that He is unable to do so, as happens to a human in the state of muteness and infancy.' The Mu'tazila said: His speech is 'the created letters and sounds, which do not subsist in His essence.' So the meaning =

Commentary
This passage introduces a three-part framework for discussing God's speech: (1) its meaning, (2) the proof of its necessity, (3) its relation (taʿalluq). It then expounds the first part by contrasting the positions of Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila. Ahl al-Sunna define speech as an eternal attribute, free from physical and psychological limitations—it is not letters or sounds, not subject to time or grammatical categories, and not a mere internal silence or inability. The Mu'tazila define it as created (ḥādith) letters and sounds that do not subsist in God's essence. The passage cuts off after presenting the Mu'tazila definition, but the contrast is clear. The text follows the common kalam method of stating the orthodox view and then the heretical view.

The Attribute of Speech: Eternal and One Despite Different Scriptures
Sanusi defines the attribute of speech and clarifies its proof by revelation, highlighting its unity across scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of speech is defined as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah’s essence, proven by revelation, and that it is one despite being expressed in different scriptures.

Core Idea
God's speech is one eternal attribute, expressed in many scriptures.

Sanusi now turns to the fifth essential attribute: speech. Unlike the previous attributes (life, knowledge, will, power), which are proven by reason, the attribute of speech is proven by revelation. In this passage, he defines speech as an eternal attribute that negates silence and defect, and explains how it is indicated through expression, writing, and indication. The key insight is that the attribute itself is one, even when its expressions vary across languages and scriptures.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the definition of the attribute of speech: it is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah’s essence, contradictory to silence and defect.
Notice that Sanusi emphasizes its eternity (azaliyya) and that it is not a temporal occurrence.
2
Understand the proof by revelation
The passage states that the proof for speech is revelation (al-sam‘), unlike the previous attributes which are proven by reason. Reflect on why revelation is necessary to affirm this attribute.
This is a key difference in kalam methodology for establishing divine attributes.
3
Reflect on the unity of the attribute
The passage gives examples of expressions in Arabic (Qur’an), Syriac (Gospel), and Hebrew (Torah), yet affirms that the named (al-musammā) is one. Consider how this preserves the unity and eternity of Allah’s speech despite different revealed books.
This refutes the idea that God’s speech is multiple or created.

Section of Divine Matters. 01 - The Attribute of Speech: Its Definition and Its Proof According to Allah Most High. 'Likewise speech' is the fifth attribute; so it is in the necessity of being described with it like the preceding attributes, although it differs from them in the manner of proof; for it contains the proof of revelation (al-sam'), while they contain the proof of reason (al-'aql). And it is: an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya), subsisting in His essence Most High, contradictory to silence and defect; by it He is commanding, forbidding, informing, and so on. It is indicated by expression, writing, and indication. When it is expressed in Arabic, it is the Qur'an; in Syriac, it is the Gospel; in Hebrew, it is the Torah. So the named (al-musammā) is one, even if the expressions differ. This is the meaning of his speech. (1) His saying: 'contradictory to silence and defect' — silence is the abandonment of speech by choice, and defect is incapacity. Al-Amir. (2) His saying: 'indication' — it is possible that what is meant is sensory indication, such that one points to the inscriptions, saying 'this is the speech of Allah', so the referent of the demonstrative pronoun is the meaning through two intermediaries. It is also valid that what is meant is intellectual indication, such that one turns in his heart to the indication and observes that the referent is the meaning subsisting in the essence. So what is meant by expression is the revealed books, and by indication is a word we use, like saying that meaning subsisting in the essence is eternal, and sufficient in indication is a certain perception. Al-Shanawani and Al-Amir. (3) His saying: 'when it is expressed' — i.e., some of its denotation. Al-Amir. (4) His saying: 'the named is one' — he intended by it the denotation in the sense of the eternal attribute. Al-Amir. (5) Discourse on the attribute of speech involves three aspects: the first is the explanation of the meaning of speech with respect to Him; the second is the proof of the necessity of Allah being described with speech; the third is the explanation of what the attribute of speech attaches to.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of speech (ṣifat al-kalām). It begins by confirming that speech is the fifth attribute among the essential attributes, and that it is necessary to describe Allah with it just like the previous attributes (life, knowledge, will, power). However, unlike those attributes which are proven by reason (al-'aql), the proof for speech is revelation (al-sam'). The author then gives the definition: speech is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya), subsisting in Allah's essence, which negates both silence (sukūt: voluntarily not speaking) and defect (āfa: incapacity to speak). Through this attribute, Allah commands, forbids, informs, etc. The passage explains that this attribute is indicated by expression (al-ʿibāra), writing (al-kitāba), and indication (al-ishāra). When these expressions vary (Arabic, Syriac, Hebrew), the named (al-musammā), i.e., the eternal attribute itself, remains one. The marginal notes clarify key points: the distinction between sensory and intellectual indication, that expression applies to the revealed books, and that the unity of the attribute is preserved despite different expressions. Finally, a note outlines three aspects of the discussion: the meaning of speech, its proof, and its objects.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's speech is multiple (different books are different speeches) or created in time.
Correction: The passage affirms that the attribute of speech is one eternal attribute; the different scriptures are expressions of that one attribute, not multiple or created entities.

Key Takeaway

?
The attribute of speech is proven by revelation, not reason. Are there verses of the Qur’an that you have been trying to rationalize away because they are difficult for you? Will you submit to them today as the very speech of the Creator?
?
If the Qur’an is the Arabic expression of Allah’s one eternal attribute, then every verse is an expression of His will for you. What change in your daily life would show that you truly believe this?

Three Schools on God's Speech
Sanusi compares the positions of Ahl al-Sunna, Mu'tazila, and Hanabila on the nature of God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major theological positions on the nature of God's speech (kalam).

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal and not composed of letters or sounds—Ahl al-Sunna's view.

In this passage, Sanusi succinctly lays out the differing definitions of God's speech held by three major schools. He begins with the Ahl al-Sunna position, then contrasts it with the Mu'tazila, and finally addresses an extreme Hanbali view. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping later debates on the Qur'an's createdness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
Read the passage and note the three groups: Ahl al-Sunna, Mu'tazila, and Hanabila. Pay attention to how Sanusi introduces each.
Sanusi uses 'قال أهل السنة', 'قال المعتزلة', and 'ينسب إلى الحنابلة'.
2
Compare Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila definitions
The Ahl al-Sunna define speech as an eternal attribute not composed of letters or sounds. The Mu'tazila define it as created letters and sounds. Notice the key contrast: eternal vs. created, and whether it subsists in God's essence.
3
Examine the Hanbali view and its extreme
The Hanabila are attributed with saying speech is arranged letters and sounds that are eternal. Some extremists claimed even the physical mushaf is eternal. Sanusi dismisses this as unbelievable.
This extreme view is often misattributed to all Hanbalis.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each definition affects the understanding of the Qur'an: is it created or uncreated? This will be important for later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا عن الوجه الأول فنقول : اختلف أهل الملل والمذاهب في معنى كلامه تعالى ، فقال أهل السنة الكلام: «صفة أزليَّة قائمة بذاته تعالى ليست بحرف ولا صوت، منزّهة عن التقدم والتأخر، والإعراب والبناء، ومنزّهة عن السكوت النفسي، بأن لا يُدِيرَ في نفسه الكلام مع القدرة عليه، ومنزّهة عن الآفة الباطنيَّة، بألا يقدر على ذلك كما يكون للآدمي في حال الخرس والطفولية
وقال المعتزلة : كلامه تعالى هو «الحروف والأصوات الحادثة ، وهي غير قائمة بذاته» ، فمعنى كونه متكلما عندهم أنه خالق للكلام في بعض الأجسام ، وهذا مبني عندهم على أن الكلام لا يكون إلا بحرف وصوت ، وهو سيأتي بيانه. ويُنسب إلى الحنابلة أنهم قالوا: إن كلامه تعالى هو «الحروف والأصوات المُترتِّبة وأنهم زعموا : أنها قديمة، وأن بعضهم تَغَالَى حتَّى زعم قدمَ هذه الحروفِ التي نقرؤها والرسوم التي نرسمها، بل ينسب لبعضهم أنه تغالى حتى ادعى القدم لغلاف المصحف وورقه، وهو بعيد التصديق، فإنه لا يتصوّر أنَّ عاقلا ينسب القِدَم لَما يُحْدِثُهُ بيده
As for the first aspect, we say: The people of religions and sects differed concerning the meaning of His speech Most High. Ahl al-Sunna said: speech is 'an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence Most High, not a letter nor a sound, transcendent of priority and posteriority, of iʿrāb and bināʾ, pure of psychic silence in that He does not turn over speech in Himself while having power over it, and pure of inward defect in that He is not incapable of that as happens to a human in the state of muteness and infancy.' The Muʿtazila said: His speech Most High is 'the created letters and sounds, and they do not subsist in His essence.' So the meaning of His being speaking according to them is that He creates speech in some bodies, and this is based on their view that speech is only by letter and sound, and its explanation will come. It is attributed to the Ḥanābila that they said: His speech Most High is 'the arranged letters and sounds,' and they claimed that they are eternal, and that some of them went to excess until they claimed the eternity of these letters which we read and the lines which we write; nay, it is attributed to some of them that they went to excess even claiming eternity for the cover of the muṣḥaf and its paper. That is far from being believed, for it is inconceivable that a rational person would ascribe eternity to what he produces with his hand.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the three aspects of the speech attribute: explaining its meaning. It states that various religious sects disagreed on this. Ahl al-Sunna define it as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, not composed of letters or sounds, free from temporal ordering (priority/posteriority), grammatical features (iʿrāb, bināʾ), and from the kind of internal speech a human might conceive. It is also free from incapacity (such as muteness). The Muʿtazila hold that God's speech consists of created letters and sounds that do not subsist in His essence; for them, God being 'speaking' means He creates speech in some body (e.g., the tree for Moses). The Ḥanābila are attributed with the view that the arranged letters and sounds are eternal, with some extremists claiming even the physical ink and paper of the Qur'an are eternal—a claim Sanusi dismisses as unbelievable, since a rational person would not call something he makes with his hand eternal.

Key Terms
كلام الله
صفة أزلية
حروف وأصوات
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Hanbalis believe the physical Qur'an (ink and paper) is eternal.
Correction: Sanusi attributes this extreme view only to some unnamed individuals among the Hanabila, and he dismisses it as unbelievable, implying it is not the mainstream Hanbali position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental difference between the Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila definitions of God's speech?
A) Ahl al-Sunna say it is created, Mu'tazila say it is eternal
B) Ahl al-Sunna say it is eternal and not composed of letters/sounds, Mu'tazila say it is created letters/sounds
C) Ahl al-Sunna say it consists of letters and sounds, Mu'tazila say it is a meaning in the soul
D) They both agree it is an eternal attribute

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna define God's speech as an eternal attribute, while the Mu'tazila define it as created letters and sounds, and some Hanbalis claimed even the physical Qur'an is eternal—a view Sanusi rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the Qur'an you recite is a created expression of an eternal speech, or do you sometimes treat the physical book as if it were eternal itself? How does this affect your reverence for it?
?
Sanusi describes the Mu'tazila as saying God's speech is created letters and sounds. If that were true, what would that imply about God's knowledge? Have you ever doubted that God's speech is eternal?
?
Consider the Hanbali extreme view that even the cover of the mushaf is eternal. Sanusi says it is unbelievable. But have you ever behaved as if the physical Qur'an were eternal, putting it on a pedestal while neglecting to understand its meaning? What change will you make today?

God's Speech: One Attribute with Conceptual Aspects
Sanusi explains God's speech is one attribute with conceptual divisions based on attachments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunna affirm that God's speech is a single attribute while allowing for diverse names like command and prohibition based on its relation to different objects.

Core Idea
God's speech is one but its divisions arise from what it relates to, not from change in God.

Sanusi here addresses a key puzzle: how can we call God's speech 'command' and 'report' without implying change or multiplicity in God? He clarifies that the diversity lies in the conceptual attachments (mutaʿallaqāt), not in the attribute itself. This passage shows the sophisticated way Sunni theologians reconcile unity and diversity in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the five divisions
List the five conceptual divisions presented: amr, nahy, khabar, wa'd, wa'id. Note what each is associated with.
These are not separate attributes but aspects of one attribute.
3
Understand 'conceptual division'
Reflect on the phrase 'aqsām iʿtibāriyya' (conceptual divisions). This means the divisions exist in our understanding, not in God's essence.
4
Connect to the core idea
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter? It safeguards God's unity while doing justice to the varied language of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأهل السنة يذهبون إلى أن كلام الله صفة واحدة لا تعدد فيها ، لكن هذه الصفة لها أقسام اعتبارية من حيث متعلقاتها ، فمن حيث تعلّقها بطلب فعل الصَّلاة مثلا: أمر ، ومِن حيث تعلقها بطلب ترك الزنى مثلا : نهي ، ومن حيث تعلقها بأن فرعون فعل كذا مثلا : خبر ، ومِن حيثُ تعلقها بأن الطائع له الجنة وَعْد، ومن حيث تعلّقها بأن العاصي يدخل النَّارِ: وَعِيدٌ، وهكذا.
And Ahl al-Sunna hold that the speech of Allah is one attribute with no multiplicity in it, but this attribute has conceptual divisions (aqsām iʿtibāriyya) in terms of its attachments (mutaʿallaqāt). So in terms of its attachment to requesting the act of prayer, for example: it is a command (amr); in terms of its attachment to requesting the abandonment of adultery, for example: it is a prohibition (nahy); in terms of its attachment to that Pharaoh did such-and-such, for example: it is a report (khabar); in terms of its attachment to that the obedient person has paradise: it is a promise (waʿd); in terms of its attachment to that the disobedient person enters the fire: it is a threat (waʿīd); and so on.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Ahl al-Sunna position that God's speech is a single, non-multiple attribute. Despite its oneness, it admits of conceptual divisions (aqsām iʿtibāriyya) based on its attachments (mutaʿallaqāt) — i.e., what it relates to. These divisions are not real multiplicities in God but relational distinctions. The author gives examples: when speech relates to requesting an act (like prayer), it is called 'command' (amr); when relating to requesting abstinence (like from adultery), it is 'prohibition' (nahy); when relating to information about the past (like Pharaoh's deed), it is 'report' (khabar); when relating to reward for obedience, it is 'promise' (waʿd); when relating to punishment for disobedience, it is 'threat' (waʿīd). This shows that the diversity arises from the objects of the speech, not from variation in the divine essence.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
خبر
وعد
وعيد
أقسام اعتبارية
متعلقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different names (command, prohibition, etc.) mean that God's speech is multiple or that God changes.
Correction: The diversity is only in the attachments (mutaʿallaqāt), not in the attribute itself. God's speech remains one and unchanging.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, what is the basis for the different names (command, prohibition, report, etc.) given to God's speech?
A) The speech is created anew for each situation
B) The speech is one but its attachments (mutaʿallaqāt) differ
C) God has multiple distinct speech attributes
D) The names are metaphorical and have no real basis

Key Takeaway
God's speech is a single eternal attribute; its variety of names reflects only the different objects it relates to, not any real diversity in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an and encounter commands like 'pray' and prohibitions like 'do not commit adultery', do you ever catch yourself thinking of these as separate 'utterances' in God's mind? How does realizing they are all one eternal attribute—only differentiated by what they relate to—change your sense of God's oneness and immutability? Take one specific command from today's prayer and silently affirm that it is not a new word from God but a relational aspect of His single, unchanging speech. What shifts in your heart?
?
The source says that the promise of paradise to the obedient and the threat of fire to the disobedient are both conceptual divisions of one speech. If you truly believe that both promise and threat are equally real aspects of the same eternal attribute, does that make you more mindful of God's justice and mercy together? Confess honestly: does your heart tend to focus more on the promise (and take it for granted) or on the threat (and feel overwhelmed)? How can this teaching recalibrate your balance?
?
You say 'Allah said' when quoting the Qur'an. Based on this lesson, what exactly is 'saying'? Is it a new act each time you recite? Or is it the same timeless attribute manifesting? How would your reverence change if, before reciting any verse, you paused to remember that you are not causing God to speak but rather connecting to His single, eternal speech? Choose one verse you will recite today and recite it with the awareness that it is a relationally-defined aspect of God's one attribute. Write down what difference that made.

The Two Meanings of Speech: Inner Speech vs. Articulated Sounds
Sanusi defines 'speech' and contrasts Mu'tazila and Sunna views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna position that the word 'speech' applies to both inner mental content and articulated sounds, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila restriction to sounds only.

Core Idea
Speech is both the inner word and the uttered word—the Mu'tazila only accept the latter.

This passage begins an important investigation into the nature of the divine attribute of speech. The author notes that this debate is so central that the entire science of theology (kalam) is named after it. Before exploring whether speech can be attributed to God, we must first define what 'speech' means. Here, Sanusi presents the two competing definitions from the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, arguing that inner speech is a real and undeniable phenomenon distinct from sounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila restrict speech to sounds and letters, while Ahl al-Sunna include inner speech.
2
Understand the concept of inner speech (حديث النفس)
Focus on the argument that inner speech is real: every person experiences composing speech in their mind, and we say 'there is speech in his soul' before utterance. This shows inner speech is not reducible to power, will, or knowledge.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider that if humans have inner speech, then God's speech—being perfect—is of the same nature but without sounds or letters. This sets the stage for the Sunni doctrine of the eternal speech of God.
The passage is incomplete but lays the foundation for the divine attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان البحث في صفة الكلام قد أخذ دورًا بعيد المدى في هذه الملة حتى إنهم سموا علم العقائد كلها : علم الكلام ، وكان مدار الخلاف على بيان حقيقة الكلام وأنه يستحيل أن تقوم هذه الحقيقة بذاته تعالى أو لا يستحيل قيامها به ، وكان الكشف عن ذلك كله بعبارة غاية في الوضوح مما تتبين معه حقيقة الخلاف ويتضح به وجه الحق ، كان علينا أن نحاول تقريب هذه المسألة بقدر ما وسعه جهدنا فنقول:
الخطوة الأولى في هذه المسألة تحديد معنى الكلام، وهل يتعيَّنُ أَلَّا يكون كلاما إلا ما كان بصوت وحرف أو لا ذلك ؟
يتعين
أما المعتزلة فذهبوا إلى أنه لا يسمى كلاماً إلا ما كان بصوت وحرف. وأما أهل السُّنَّة فقالوا: يطلق لفظ الكلام على شيئين: الأول: حديثُ النَّفس، والثاني: الأصوات والحروف المُترتِّبة ، ولا سبيل إلى إنكار المعنى الأول في حقٌّ الإنسان، ولا في أنه يُسمى كلاماً ، ولا في أنَّه زائد على القدرة والإرادة والعلم، ألا تری أن الإنسان يقول: زورت البارحة في نفسي كلاماً ، وأنا نقول في نفس فلان كلام وهو يريد أن ينطق به ، فأما أن ذلك ثابت في حق الإنسان، فإن كل واحد منا يشعر من نفسه به ، وأما أنه يسمى كلاما=
And since the research on the attribute of speech had taken a far-reaching role in this community, so much so that they named the entire science of beliefs as 'the science of kalām (ʿilm al-kalām)', and the pivot of the disagreement was on explaining the reality of speech and whether it is impossible for this reality to subsist in His essence Most High or not impossible for it to subsist in Him, and the uncovering of all that with an expression of utmost clarity by which the reality of the disagreement would be apparent and the face of truth would become clear, it was incumbent upon us to try to bring this issue close to understanding as far as our effort allowed, so we say: The first step in this issue is the determination of the meaning of speech, and whether it is determined that it cannot be speech except that which is with sound and letter, or not that. Determined? As for the Muʿtazila, they went to the view that nothing is called speech except what is with sound and letter. As for Ahl al-Sunna, they said: the word 'speech' is applied to two things: first: inner speech (ḥadīth al-nafs), and second: the arranged sounds and letters. There is no way to deny the first meaning in the case of humans, nor that it is called speech, nor that it is additional to power, will, and knowledge. Do you not see that a person says: 'I composed speech in myself last night,' and we say: 'There is speech in so-and-so's soul' while he wants to utter it? As for that being established in the case of humans, every one of us is aware of it from himself, and as for it being called speech... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage introduces the broader inquiry into the nature of the divine attribute of speech. The author notes that this debate was so central that the entire science of beliefs was named after it: ʿilm al-kalām. The key question is whether it is possible for speech to subsist in God's essence. To clarify, the author proposes a first step: define what 'speech' means. The Muʿtazila restrict the term to sounds and letters. Ahl al-Sunna, however, argue that the word 'speech' has two meanings: (1) inner speech (ḥadīth al-nafs) — the mental content before articulation, and (2) articulate sounds and letters. They argue that the first meaning is undeniable in humans: we are directly aware of composing speech in our minds, and we say 'there is speech in so-and-so's soul' meaning intended speech not yet uttered. This inner speech is not reducible to power, will, or knowledge but is something additional. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clearly leading to the conclusion that if humans have inner speech, then God's eternal speech is the perfect form of this attribute, without letters or sounds.

كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some deny that inner speech is a real entity and only accept articulated speech as genuine speech.
Correction: Sanusi argues that inner speech is directly experienced by every person and is commonly called 'speech' in everyday language, establishing its reality.

Checkpoint

A) Command and prohibition
B) Inner speech (حديث النفس) and arranged sounds/letters

D) Letters and sounds only

Key Takeaway
Speech includes both inner mental content and articulated sounds; the Mu'tazila error is restricting it to only the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What will you do the next time a corrupt thought arises?
?

How does Sanusi's argument that you are directly aware of inner speech in yourself force you to take your innermost intentions more seriously before Allah?
?
Sanusi points out that inner speech is additional to power, will, and knowledge. How will you respond when an uninvited thought enters your mind from now on?

The Mu'tazilite and Extreme Hanbali Views on God's Speech
Describes the Mu'tazilite and Hanbali views on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite and extreme Hanbali views on God's speech, and why the latter's claim of eternal letters and physical objects is considered irrational.

Core Idea
Some claimed the very ink and paper of the Qur'an are eternal.

This passage presents two problematic views about God's speech. The Mu'tazilites held that God creates speech in bodies, while an extreme Hanbali view asserted that God's speech consists of eternal letters and sounds, with some even claiming the physical mushaf is eternal. The author dismisses the latter as far-fetched.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the passage and note the Mu'tazilite and Hanbali positions. The Mu'tazilites say God creates speech in bodies; the Hanbalis say it is literal letters and sounds.
The Hanbali view is the main focus of criticism.
2
Examine the extreme claims
Notice the escalation: from letters being eternal to the physical cover and paper being eternal. The author finds this hard to believe.
3
Understand the dismissal
Reflect on why the author calls the extreme view 'far-fetched' and why it is rationally unsustainable.
The key point: it is impossible for a rational person to claim that what they themselves produce is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه ـ صفة الكلام، تعريفها، وجهة ثبوتها الله تعالى كونه متكلما عندهم أنه خالق للكلام في بعض الأجسام ، وهذا مبني عندهم على أن الكلام لا يكون إلا بحرف وصوت ، وهو سيأتي بيانه. ويُنسب إلى الحنابلة أنهم قالوا: إن كلامه تعالى هو «الحروف والأصوات المُترتِّبة وأنهم زعموا : أنها قديمة، وأن بعضهم تَغَالَى حتَّى زعم قدمَ هذه الحروفِ التي نقرؤها والرسوم التي نرسمها، بل ينسب لبعضهم أنه تغالى حتى ادعى القدم لغلاف المصحف وورقه، وهو بعيد التصديق، فإنه لا يتصوّر أنَّ عاقلا ينسب القِدَم لَما يُحْدِثُهُ بيده [ وانظر كلام المصنف في شرحه «هداية المريد» لمزيد فائدة في هذا المقام ، ج۱ / ص ۳۸۸ وما بعدها ] . وأهل السنة يذهبون إلى أن كلام الله صفة واحدة لا تعدد فيها ، لكن هذه الصفة لها أقسام اعتبارية من حيث متعلقاتها ، فمن حيث تعلّقها بطلب فعل الصَّلاة مثلا: أمر ، ومِن حيث تعلقها بطلب ترك الزنى مثلا : نهي ، ومن حيث تعلقها بأن فرعون فعل كذا مثلا: خبر ، ومِن حيثُ تعلقها بأن الطائع له الجنة وَعْد، ومن حيث تعلّقها بأن العاصي يدخل النَّارِ: وَعِيدٌ، وهكذا.
خمسة: صفة الكلام، تعريفها، وجهة ثبوتها: كونه متكلما عندهم (the Mu'tazila) أنه خالق للكلام في بعض الأجسام، وهذا مبني عندهم على أن الكلام لا يكون إلا بحرف وصوت، وسيأتي بيانه. ويُنسب إلى الحنابلة أنهم قالوا: إن كلامه تعالى هو «الحروف والأصوات المُترتِّبة» وأنهم زعموا أنها قديمة، وأن بعضهم تَغَالَى حتَّى زعم قدمَ هذه الحروفِ التي نقرؤها والرسوم التي نرسمها، بل ينسب لبعضهم أنه تغالى حتى ادعى القدم لغلاف المصحف وورقه، وهو بعيد التصديق، فإنه لا يتصوّر أنَّ عاقلا ينسب القِدَم لَما يُحْدِثُهُ بيده [ وانظر كلام المصنف في شرحه «هداية المريد» لمزيد فائدة في هذا المقام ، ج۱ / ص ۳۸۸ وما بعدها ] . وأهل السنة يذهبون إلى أن كلام الله صفة واحدة لا تعدد فيها، لكن هذه الصفة لها أقسام اعتبارية من حيث متعلقاتها، فمن حيث تعلّقها بطلب فعل الصَّلاة مثلا: أمر، ومِن حيث تعلقها بطلب ترك الزنى مثلا: نهي، ومن حيث تعلقها بأن فرعون فعل كذا مثلا: خبر، ومِن حيثُ تعلقها بأن الطائع له الجنة وَعْد، ومن حيث تعلّقها بأن العاصي يدخل النَّارِ: وَعِيدٌ، وهكذا.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage introduces the attribute of speech (صفة الكلام). It begins by noting the Mu'tazilite view that God creates speech in some bodies, based on their claim that speech consists only of letters and sounds. Then it mentions the Hanbali position attributed to them: that God's speech is actual letters and sounds, and some extremists claimed they are eternal, even the physical cover and paper of the Qur'an. The author dismisses this as far-fetched. Next, it presents the Sunni (Ash'ari) doctrine: God's speech is a single, undivided attribute, but it has multiple relational aspects (أقسام اعتبارية) depending on its objects. Examples: when related to commanding an action like prayer, it is called command (أمر); when related to prohibiting an act like adultery, it is prohibition (نهي); when related to informing about Pharaoh's deeds, it is information (خبر); when related to promising paradise for the obedient, it is promise (وعد); when related to threatening hell for the disobedient, it is threat (وعيد).

Key Terms
صفة الكلام
الحروف والأصوات
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what did the extreme Hanbalis claim about the letters of the Qur'an?
A) They are created.
B) They are eternal.
C) They are metaphors.
D) They are non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The extreme Hanbali claim that letters and physical objects are eternal is rejected as irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a physical copy of the Quran with such reverence that it became more about the object than the message? How can you recalibrate your respect to focus on the eternal speech of Allah rather than the paper and ink?
?
When you encounter a belief about Islam that seems extreme or irrational, like the claim that the physical Quran is eternal, do you examine it with critical thinking or simply accept it out of loyalty? What step will you take this week to verify a belief you hold?
?
The author dismisses an extreme view as something 'no rational person would believe.' Are there areas of your faith where you cling to emotionally appealing ideas despite rational evidence against them? How can you pursue truth even when it challenges your comfort?

The Sunni View: One Attribute of Speech with Multiple Aspects
Presents the Sunni doctrine of a singular attribute of speech with relational multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that God's speech is a single attribute with multiple relational aspects such as command, prohibition, information, promise, and threat.

Core Idea
God's speech is one, but it becomes command, prohibition, news, promise, or threat depending on what it relates to.

After dismissing extreme views, the author presents the Sunni position: God's speech is a single, undivided attribute. However, this one attribute appears as command, prohibition, information, promise, or threat when it relates to different things. This elegant solution avoids both literalism and denial of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the statement that speech is one attribute with no multiplicity.
The phrase 'صفة واحدة لا تعدد فيها' is key.
2
Identify the relational aspects
The passage lists five aspects: command, prohibition, information, promise, threat. Note how each is defined by what the speech relates to.
These are not separate attributes but the same attribute in relation to different things.
3
Understand the concept of اعتبارية
The divisions are conceptual, not real. The attribute itself is one, but our understanding of it differs based on its object.
This resolves the problem of multiple names for God's speech.

The Sunni Analogy with Human Inner Speech: A Clarification
Clarifies that the Sunni analogy to human inner speech is only to refute Mu'tazila, not to equate essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna's analogy between God's speech and human inner speech is exclusively for refuting the Mu'tazila, not for asserting any essential similarity.

Core Idea
The only commonality between God's speech and ours is that both are not letters or sounds — nothing more.

This passage is a safeguard against a serious misunderstanding. When Sunni scholars compare God's eternal speech to human inner speech, they do so solely to counter the Mu'tazila who claim all speech is composed of letters and sounds. The analogy is a refutation tool, not a claim of ontological likeness. Understanding this distinction prevents falling into anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the author's repetition of the purpose: 'mere refutation'.
The author is very explicit about the limited scope of the analogy.
2
Identify the purpose of the analogy
Find where the author states that the purpose is only 'الرد على المعتزلة' (refutation of the Mu'tazila). Understand that it is not to describe God's speech positively.
3
Understand why essential similarity is impossible
The author gives a reason: human inner speech is a created accident (عرض حادث) with temporal sequence. This shows it cannot be like God's eternal speech.
This refutes any claim of essential commonality.
4
Grasp the negative commonality
The only shared attribute is negative: both are not letters nor sounds. The author calls this 'الصفة السلبية'. Note that even this is not a positive similarity.
The realities remain completely distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبل أن ننتقل من الكلام على هذه الخطوة، نقرّر لك أنَّ غرض أهل السُّنَّة في هذا الموضع مِن تشبيه كلامه تعالى بكلامنا النَّفسي هو مجرَّد الرَّدَّ على المعتزلة القائلين بأنَّ الكلام منحصر في ذي الحروف والأصوات، وليس مرادهم تشبیه کلامه جل وعزّ بكلامنا النَّفسي في الكنه والحقيقة، وكيف يُتوهم أن كلامه تعالى مماثل لكلامنا النَّفسي مع أن كلامنا النَّفسي أيضا أعراض حادثة يوجد فيها التَّقدُّم والتأخرُ ، وطرو بعضه بعد عدم بعضه، ويوجد شيئا فشيئًا ؟ فمن توهم ذلك في كلامه تعالى فليس بينه وبين الحشوية ونحوهم من المبتدعة القائلين بأنَّ كلامه تعالى حروف وأصوات فرق ! وإنما مقصد العلماء بذكر الكلام النَّفسيّ في الشَّاهد ، النَّقض على المعتزلة في حصرهم الكلام في ذي الحروف والأصوات، فيقال لهم : يَنتَقِصُ هذا الحصر بكلامنا النَّفسي ؛ فإنَّه كلام حقيقة وليس بحرفٍ ولا صوتٍ ، وإذا صح ذلك فكلام مولانا كلام مع أنه ليس بحرف ولا صوت، فلم يقع الاشتراك بين كلام الباري وكلامنا النَّفسي إِلَّا في هذه الصفة السلبية، وهي: أنَّ كُلًّا منهما ليس بحرف ولا صوت ، أما الحقيقة فمباينة للحقيقة كلَّ المُباينة ، فَاعْرِف هذا وكُن منه على ثَبَتٍ.
And before we move on from the discussion of this step, we affirm for you that the purpose of Ahl al-Sunna in this place in likening His speech, exalted, to our inner speech (كلامنا النفسي) is merely the refutation (الرد) of the Mu'tazila who say that speech is limited to what possesses letters and sounds. They do not intend to liken His speech, mighty and majestic, to our inner speech in essence (الكنه) and reality (الحقيقة). How could it be imagined that His speech, exalted, is similar to our inner speech, while our inner speech is also accidental [؟] attributes (أعراض حادثة) in which there is temporal precedence and posteriority, and some of it occurs after the non-existence of some, and it occurs little by little? Whoever imagines that in His speech, exalted, there is no difference between him and the Hashwiyya and their like among the heretics (المبتدعة) who say that His speech, exalted, is letters and sounds! Rather, the purpose of the scholars in mentioning inner speech in the witnessed world (الشاهد) is to invalidate (النقض) the Mu'tazila in their limiting speech to what possesses letters and sounds. So it is said to them: 'This limitation is invalidated (يَنتَقِصُ) by our inner speech, for it is truly speech (كلام حقيقة) without being a letter or sound. And if that is correct, then the speech of our Lord is speech while it is not a letter or sound. So the commonality (الاشتراك) between the speech of the Creator (الباري) and our inner speech occurred only in this negative attribute (الصفة السلبية), which is: that each of them is not a letter nor a sound. As for the reality (الحقيقة), it is completely different (مباينة للحقيقة كل المباينة). So know this, and be firm upon it.'
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and safeguard against misunderstanding. The author stresses that when Ahl al-Sunna compare God's speech to human inner speech (كلامنا النفسي), the sole purpose is to refute the Mu'tazila who limit speech to letters and sounds. They do not intend any positive similarity in essence. Human inner speech is itself a created accident (عرض حادث) with temporal sequence, whereas God's speech is eternal and not an accident. To imagine that God's speech is like human inner speech in reality would put one in the same category as the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists who say God's speech is letters and sounds. The commonality between God's speech and human inner speech is only a negative attribute: both lack letters and sounds. Their realities are completely distinct. The author concludes by urging the reader to understand this firmly.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الرد
الكنه
الحقيقة
الصفة السلبية
الاشتراك
الحشوية
المبتدعة
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some mistakenly think the Sunni analogy implies God's speech is similar to human inner speech in essence.
Correction: The analogy is only for refutation; the only commonality is the negative attribute of being without letters/sounds, while realities are completely distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the only commonality between God's speech and human inner speech according to this passage?
A) They are both eternal
B) They are both sounds and letters
C) They are both not letter nor sound
D) They are both accidents

Key Takeaway
The Sunni analogy is a refutation tool, not a statement of ontological equality; the only common ground is a shared absence of letters and sounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear that God's speech is not letters or sounds, do you secretly wish it were more tangible so you could feel closer? What does that reveal about your reliance on sensory experience in matters of faith?
?
Have you ever taken an analogy about Allah further than it was intended because it made you feel more comfortable? Will you now commit to stopping at the very boundary set by the scholars, even if it leaves you with mystery?
?
If the only thing your speech shares with God's is an absence, does that shrink or expand your awe of Him? How will you train your heart to be content with what He has revealed without adding or subtracting?

The Sunni Use of Kalam Nafsi to Refute the Mu'tazila
Author explains the Sunni use of kalam nafsi to refute the Mu'tazila's limitation of kalam to sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific polemical purpose of the Sunni doctrine of kalam nafsi and why it does not imply any literal similarity between Allah's speech and human inner speech.

Core Idea
Kalam nafsi is a refutational tool, not a positive analogy.

In the debate over the divine attribute of speech, the Mu'tazila argued that speech is nothing but letters and sounds. Sunni theologians countered by pointing to human inner speech (kalam nafsi), which is real speech without letters or sounds. But as this passage makes clear, the comparison is purely negative: it only shows that speech can exist without sound, not that Allah's speech resembles human inner speech in essence. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid falling into anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sunni purpose
Read the opening sentence. It states that the sole purpose of likening Allah's speech to inner speech is to refute the Mu'tazila. Note that it is not a claim of real similarity.
Purpose is polemical, not doctrinal.
2
Contrast with Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila limit speech to letters and sounds. The Sunni response uses inner speech to break that confinement.
3
Understand the negative nature
Find where the author explicitly denies any similarity in essence or reality. The passage says 'How could it be imagined...?' and warns that imagining similarity leads to the Hashwiyya error.
Emphasizes complete difference in reality.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only commonality is a negative attribute: neither is letters or sounds. The realities are utterly distinct. The author ends with a strong command to know this and be firm.
Affirms the lesson's core idea.
